Qiyas in Rational Matters: The Causality Debate
Debate on qiyas in rational matters and methods of establishing causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi argument for qiyas in rational matters by analyzing the dispute over causality and evaluating the opposing claim that only induction suffices.

Core Idea
Qiyas applies to rational matters because reason can establish cause independently, just like in shar'i matters.

This passage tackles a fundamental debate in usul: does analogical reasoning (qiyas) work in rational matters like natural causation? Opponents argue that only empirical induction (istiqra') can prove causality, rendering qiyas useless. The author defends the Hanafi position that reason, using methods like sabr (eliminative induction), can independently establish the cause in the original case, and once established, it can be generalized to new cases—just as in shar'i matters. This lesson dissects the opposing arguments, the Hanafi response, and the methodological stakes of the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
Find the argument against qiyas in rational matters. Opponents say that qiyas cannot establish causality because the unity of the ratio legis (manat) between original and branch is impossible. For example, to prove heat in grapes by analogy to honey, one cannot rely on the commonality of sweetness because heat might not be caused by sweetness in all cases; only full induction (checking every sweet thing) suffices.
Note: istiqra' here means complete empirical enumeration, which is often impractical.
2
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The author rejects the claim that causality is only established by induction. He argues that reason can independently establish the cause in the original case through sabr (eliminative induction) and other methods, as practiced by rational thinkers (theologians and philosophers). This means that once the cause is known, qiyas can transfer the ruling to similar cases.
Sabr is a method of testing attributes; if sweetness is the only common attribute that plausibly causes heat, it becomes the effective cause.
3
Examine the Taysir objection
The author cites al-Taysir to address a subtlety: if the cause is proven by a proof that abstracts from the specific locus (i.e., a general proof), then that proof itself directly applies to the branch, making qiyas unnecessary. If the proof is specific to the locus, qiyas cannot transfer the ruling. But the author dismisses this worry, asserting that in rational matters the ruling of the original can be apparent (zahir) and the cause can be generalized.
This shows the author's commitment to the unity of method between rational and shar'i realms.
4
Evaluate the distinction between rational and shar'i causes
The author calls the distinction between rational and shar'i causes 'arbitrary' (tahakkum). He argues that in both spheres, once the cause is established, generalization follows. There is no need to treat rational matters differently.
This is a strong claim that unifies the epistemology of qiyas across all domains.
5
Confirm the tarjih
The author ends with 'so understand' (fafham), indicating his preferred position is that qiyas is valid in rational matters. The Hanafi school thus accepts analogical reasoning in natural science and metaphysics, not just law.
This position has implications for how Hanafi usulis engage with rational sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل: لا يجري) القياس ( في العقليات أصلاً لعدم إمكان اتحاد المناط) بین الأصل والفرع (فلو أثبت حرارة حلو) كالعنب (قياساً على العسل) مثلاً بجامع الحلاوة (لا تثبت عليه الحلاوة إلا بالاستقراء بأن يستقري كل ما فيه حلاوة فيوجد فيه الحرارة فيعلم أن المقتضي هو الحلاوة (فتثبت الحرارة (فيه) أي في الحلو المقيس وهو العنب (به) أي بالاستقراء (لا) بالقياس فلا أصل ولا فرع هناك (أقول) لا أسلم أن علية العلة لا تثبت إلا بالاستقراء، بل (العقل قد يستبد بإثبات المناط في الأصل فقط بالسبر (وغيره) من المسالك (كما عليه العقلاء من المتكلمين والحكماء) وقال في التيسير»: لو ثبت بدليل آخر فذلك الدليل يكفي في إثبات المطلوب، لأن مدلول ذلك الدليل هو علية الحلاوة للحرارة مجرّدة عن محل مخصوص هو الأصل، فهو يكفي لإثبات الحرارة في الفرع وضاع الأصل، أو مدلوله عليتها في المحل المخصوص، وحينئذ لا يصح القياس فإنه لا يوجب تعدية الحكم من الأصل إلى الفرع، وهذا بخلاف العلل الشرعية، لأن النصوص توجب عليتها بالنسبة إلى محل مخصوص، ثم تجرد عن الخصوص فيتعدى إلى غيره وأنت تعلم أن الفرق تحكم، بل يجوز أن يكون حكم الأصل ظاهراً في العقليات وتثبت العلة بدليلها فيعمم بتعميم العلة كما في الشرعيات بعينه فافهم
And it was said: Qiyas does not run in rational matters at all because of the impossibility of the unity of the ratio legis between the original and the branch. For if one establishes heat in a sweet thing like grapes by analogy to honey, for example, by the commonality of sweetness, heat is not established in it except by induction: by examining every sweet thing and finding heat in it, so one knows that the causative factor is the sweetness. So heat is established in it (i.e., the sweet thing being measured, which is grapes) by it (i.e., by induction), not by qiyas; there is no original or branch there. (I say:) I do not concede that causality of a cause is only established by induction. Rather, reason can independently establish the ratio legis in the original only through examination (sabr) and other methods, as is the practice of rational thinkers among theologians and philosophers. And it is said in al-Taysir: If it is established by another proof, that proof suffices for establishing the required ruling, because the signification of that proof is the causality of sweetness for heat stripped of the specific locus that is the original; it suffices to establish heat in the branch, and the original is wasted. Or its signification is causality in the specific locus, and then qiyas is not valid because it does not necessitate the transference of the ruling from the original to the branch. This is unlike shar'i causes, because texts necessitate their causality with respect to a specific locus, then they are stripped of particularity and extend to others. And you know that the difference is arbitrary. Rather, it is permissible that the ruling of the original be apparent in rational matters and the cause be established by its proof, and then generalization occurs by generalization of the cause, just as in shar'i matters exactly, so understand.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage addresses the applicability of qiyas (analogical reasoning) to rational matters (al-'aqliyyat), such as natural causation. Some scholars deny qiyas in rational matters because the unity of the ratio legis (manat) between original and branch is not reliably known. They give the example: if one wants to prove that a sweet substance (like grapes) has heat by analogy to honey based on the commonality of sweetness, this cannot be done by qiyas because heat is not necessarily caused by sweetness; only through induction (istiqra') can one empirically establish that all sweet things are hot. The author refutes this, arguing that reason can establish causality in the original through methods like sabr (eliminative induction) and other techniques, as used by theologians and philosophers. He cites al-Taysir to argue that if a cause is established by a proof that abstracts from the specific locus, then that proof directly applies to the branch, making qiyas redundant. But if the proof is specific to the locus, then qiyas is invalid because it does not transfer the ruling. However, the author dismisses the alleged difference between rational and shar'i causes as arbitrary (tahakkum). He asserts that in rational matters, the ruling of the original can be apparent (zahir), and once the cause is established, it can be generalized just as in shar'i matters. Thus, qiyas is valid in rational matters as well.

Key Terms
السبر (al-sabr): Eliminative induction: a method of determining the cause by testing and eliminating irrelevant attributes.
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Inductive reasoning by examining all instances of a class to establish a universal rule.
اتحاد المناط (ittihad al-manat): Unity of the ratio legis: the common attribute between original and branch that justifies the analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what method does the author accept for establishing the causal link in rational matters?
A) Only full induction (istiqra').
B) Reason independently through sabr and other methods.
C) Reliance on divine revelation only.
D) Accepting the impossibility of establishing causes altogether.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms qiyas in rational matters because reason can independently establish causes through methods like sabr, and the distinction between rational and shar'i causes is rejected as arbitrary.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
beginner
8 min
Condition: The Original's Proof Must Not Cover the Branch
Condition for valid qiyas: dalil must not include branch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a qiyas is invalid because the original's proof already covers the branch, and explain why it is considered arbitrary (tahakkum).

Core Idea
If the proof of the original directly covers the branch, qiyas becomes an unnecessary exercise.

This lesson examines a key procedural condition for valid qiyas: the dalil (proof) that establishes the ruling of the original (asl) must not already encompass the branch (far'). If it does, then resorting to qiyas is pointless and considered arbitrary. Understanding this condition sharpens your ability to critically evaluate qiyas arguments in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
State the condition
Identify the rule: the proof of the original must not include the ruling of the branch. If it does, qiyas is invalid.
2
Understand the consequence
If the branch is already covered, using qiyas is called 'tahakkum' (arbitrary) and 'tatwil bila ta'il' (useless prolongation). The derivation is redundant.
The passage emphasizes that this renders the qiyas void.
3
Work through the example
Consider analogizing gypsum (jiss) to wheat (dharra) based on the commonality of being measured (kayl) for riba. The hadith 'لا تبيعوا الدرهم بدرهمين، ولا الصاع بالصاعين' prohibits riba in measured items. Since gypsum is also measured, the hadith directly covers it, making the qiyas unnecessary.
The hadith's wording ('الصاع') is a vessel for measurement, implying all measured items.
4
Derive the principle
The proof of the cause ('illa) must be specific to the original and not lexically include the branch. If the text is general ('amm) and covers the branch, qiyas is invalid.
This is a Hanafi usul principle: the text must not be capable of directly covering the branch.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Test: Can we analogize apples (not staple food) to wheat for riba prohibition using the same measurement 'illa? The hadith explicitly mentions 'sa'', which is a measure for staple foods. Apples are not staple, so the text does not directly cover them; qiyas would be valid.
The key is whether the text's wording or scope inherently includes the branch.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومنها أن لا يكون دليله أي حكم الأصل (شاملاً لحكم الفرع (وإلا أي وإن كان شاملاً (كان) إثباته بالقياس دون دليل الأصل (تحكماً وتطويلاً بلا طائل مثلاً إذا قاس الجص على الذرة بجامع الكيل في حكم الربوية ثم أثبت ربوية الذرة بحديث لا تبيعوا الدرهم بدرهمين، ولا الصاع بالصاعين فيمكن أن يثبت ربوية الجص الحديث ويكون القياس تطويلاً من غير طائل (ومن ههنا يعلم أن دليل العلة إذا كان نصاً وجب أن لا يتناول الفرع (لفظاً) بحيث يخرج حكمه منه وإلا ضاع القياس ويكون تطويلاً من غير طائر فافهم
(And among them is) that the proof of the original (i.e., the ruling of the original) should not include the ruling of the branch. And if it does (i.e., if it includes it), then establishing it by qiyas instead of the original's proof is arbitrary and useless prolongation. For example, if one analogizes gypsum (jiss) to wheat (dharra) by the commonality of being measured in the ruling of riba, and then establishes the riba of wheat by the hadith: 'Do not sell a dirham for two dirhams, nor a sa' for two sa',' then the hadith could also establish the riba of gypsum, and the qiyas would be a prolonged useless exercise. (And from here it is known that if the proof of the cause is a text, it must not include the branch in wording such that its ruling is derived from it; otherwise, the qiyas is wasted and becomes useless prolongation, so understand.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage states a procedural condition for a valid qiyas: the proof (dalil) that establishes the original's ruling must not already directly include the branch (far'). If it does, then resorting to qiyas is arbitrary (tahakkum) and a wasteful prolongation. The example: if someone tries to prove that gypsum (jiss) is ribawi by analogy to wheat (dharra) based on the commonality of being measured (kayl), and then proves that wheat is ribawi by the hadith 'do not sell a sa' for two sa',' the same hadith could directly prove that gypsum is ribawi because it is also a measured commodity. The qiyas would then be unnecessary. Therefore, the text's proof must be specific to the original and not lexically (lafzan) include the branch. This ensures that qiyas serves a real purpose in extending a ruling when there is no direct textual coverage.

Key Terms
التحكم (al-tahakkum): Arbitrary assertion without proper evidence; a qiyas that is unnecessary due to direct textual coverage is considered arbitrary.
تطويل بلا طائل (tatwil bila ta'il): Useless prolongation: using qiyas when a direct text already covers the case, resulting in an unnecessary extra step.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is qiyas considered 'useless prolongation' if the original's proof also covers the branch?
A) Because the proof is weak.
B) Because the branch can be directly ruled by that proof without qiyas.
C) Because the original and branch are the same.
D) Because qiyas is only for non-textual matters.

Key Takeaway
A valid qiyas requires that the proof of the original does not already encompass the branch; otherwise, the analogy is redundant and arbitrary.
